To Smith, the sale was a clear sign of a cash crunch that one of GE’s biggest rivals in the power business clearly didn’t want anyone to know about.

The deals team dug down further. Alstom was heavily bloated by American standards, GE’s analysis found. Compared to GE’s Power business, Alstom had four times as many workers for every dollar of revenue it brought in. And yet, though it seemed to be in need of cash, it wasn’t laying off workers or closing facilities. Layoffs and closures—restructuring, in accounting speak—are cost-cutting measures taken with an eye to the long term, but because of costs like workers’ severance, they require cash to complete.

It seemed to Smith that Alstom would be a prime target for the cost synergies that GE loved, namely, excess workers and factory “rooftops” that could be shed to boost profit margins. But the company was so dangerously low on cash that it couldn’t do its own restructuring, not even to save itself. Buying Alstom to restructure its operations and fold it into GE’s own Power unit would make sense—but only if it could be had for the right price. Any bid needed to be carefully and precisely calculated; overpaying for a limping business could hamper a buyer for years.

Smith watched his screens for days, waiting for a sell-off in Alstom shares to drive down the company’s stock price. It was only a matter of time before the market saw what he had seen, and the moment that happened would be the right time to swoop in with a lowball offer to take some of the company’s business units off management’s hands.

But the big sell-off never came. Instead, Alstom’s shares dipped, but then soon started to climb again as investors speculated about the company’s plans. The market seemed to think that Alstom had raised the cash for a big move of its own. Regardless, other investors weren’t seeing what Smith was seeing—a big industrial company quietly trying to manage some big internal problems.

Smith and his colleagues sent their pitch to the business development team for the entire corporation at GE headquarters in Fairfield. But corporate filed the report away for the time being. Something was obviously wrong with Alstom. Perhaps one day they would find out how bad it was.



It was April 2013, late in the evening, and Jimmy Lee was in a bar on Bourbon Street, finally getting his chance to meet the new deal guy for General Electric. Lee, the irrepressible deal fanatic of JPMorgan Chase, was the kind of fellow who could happily extemporize at length about 150 percent of the transactions that might happen in any given year on Wall Street. But GE, in recent years, had been a frustratingly sparse source of deals—and bankers’ fees—for Jimmy Lee and Morgan.

GE was just the sort of sluggish and complicated colossus that Lee believed, with every fiber of his being, would benefit the most from the skillful slicing and dicing of the nation’s most august investment bank. GE was, to a banker’s mind, a web of locks to be picked. Unlock them, and all-important “value” flowed out: higher returns for the rest of the company that remained, a shot at a more focused business and profits for the unit that was sold off, a surge in trading by investors eager to see something change, and a slew of fees for the bankers and lawyers who turned the notion of the deal into a reality.

It was no surprise, then, that a furiously energetic, upbeat banker like Lee could find a willing audience in a chief executive like Immelt, who was so eager to transform—and to get credit for transforming—his massive portfolio without giving up the otherworldly scale of a conglomerate in a world from which conglomerates had largely disappeared. Lee had already helped prod Immelt into selling GE’s stake in NBC Universal to Comcast, a massive two-part deal. Now, in the spring of 2013, he saw a chance to bond with the new head of business development, just arrived from a posting in India, to impress on him the value that JPMorgan Chase—and in particular Jimmy Lee—were prepared to unlock for General Electric.

So James B. Lee and John Flannery, along with Geoff Beattie, a member of the board of directors, settled into the bar.

Lee’s message was about playing defense. Activist investors were looking for bigger and bigger targets, he said. What defenses was GE preparing? What would they do to fend off an attempt, however unexpected, from an interloper that might try to buy into GE’s stock and force management’s hand about changing its portfolio?

“Look, man,” Jimmy Lee said, “there’s some big trends going on here and you’ve got to be dialed in to it.”

Flannery cut Lee off. They had an idea already in motion, he told the banker. GE was ready to cut a profitable chunk of GE Capital loose, throwing a little over the side to show investors that they were serious about reducing its dependency on the financial services unit that had led to such lingering disgust.

Actually, a giant spin-off was in the making. What had been a unit of GE Capital that funded store-branded credit cards and layaway financing plans at outlets like Walmart would become its own company, with shares of stock swapped out to GE shareholders. By creating a new public company, Synchrony Financial, out of the old GE Capital, GE would lose the money it had been making from the unit, but it would also shave off some of its financial risks.

To no one’s surprise, when the move went public, Jimmy Lee and JPMorgan Chase were in on the deal.



Then, in late 2013, the word went out to the business development teams at all GE units: send your biggest industrial deal ideas to Fairfield. There, on GE’s blandly imposing corporate campus, Jeff Immelt and his lieutenants were in the mood to do something big. Incremental gestures toward reorienting the company—even relatively large deals like Immelt’s acquisitions in oil and gas—weren’t convincing the market that GE was really serious about kicking its reliance on finance.

In the years since the financial crisis, the consensus view of GE had hardened into what had once been the wry, cynical view: America’s most famous industrial company was actually more of a bank with profitable jet engine and power businesses grafted onto it. And being viewed as a bank spelled trouble for one of the most important metrics confronting Immelt and the other GE executives: the multiple.

The multiple, or price-to-earnings ratio, is a crude metric used to find the relative value of a company’s stock. In its most basic form, the stock price is divided by the annual per-share earnings to determine how much investors are paying per dollar of company profit. Investors tend to pay up for earnings if they think a lot more are coming; they pay less for slower-growth companies or for those with an uncertain future. Unlike the share price, the multiple allows comparisons to similar companies.

For all of GE’s growing reliance on financial services profits in the Welch years to drive its earnings up, the company had enjoyed the benefit of a double standard. It raked in cash from its lending business, just as big banks did, but investors trusted it for the stolid and secure industrial business it also was. GE had the high-efficiency profit margins of leveraged finance, but the price-to-earnings multiple on its stock price was that of a much less risky industrial conglomerate, where sales are forecasted out by years, not just quarters. Huge backlogs of heavy equipment orders and service contracts had made the company confident that even sharp economic downturns would be manageable and require no deeply painful moves. Its rich dividend in particular seemed safe from reductions.

GE’s financial crisis swoon and slow recovery had changed all that. The black box of GE Capital had been acceptable to a certain cohort of big investors, sell-side analysts, and financial journalists for as long as it remained a caveat to the larger story—that of an impregnable industrial company with a knack for setting and beating targets for Wall Street. When a real financial calamity occurred, GE Capital—along with the belated panic on the part of investors about how little they understood its inner workings—had nearly taken the entire company down. Although Keith Sherin in particular had helped to stanch the bleeding in the now-legendary investor meeting with GE analysts in March 2009, during the depths of GE’s crisis, and its stock price was back from its single-digit lows, there was one thing that wasn’t coming back: that irksome multiple.

GE’s price-to-earnings ratio now clung stubbornly to the levels of other financial services companies. To investors, the risk in GE shares was more comparable to JPMorgan Chase and Wells Fargo than to industrials like United Technologies and 3M.

For Immelt, the unchanged multiple represented a series of short-term problems. Millions of GE stock options earned as compensation by managers, executives, and board directors, both current and retired, remained underwater. Nevertheless, GE’s communications department took pains to instruct reporters that Immelt, his lieutenants like Sherin and Jeff Bornstein, and the rest of the leadership team paid no attention to the share price.

This assertion was easily contradicted over cups of coffee with GE people in Midtown, and in phone calls to offices around the country. The stock price was a daily grade of Immelt’s and the company’s performance that could bring on a lambasting by Jim Cramer and various annoyingly well-read financial columns and blogs. Such criticism could be dismissed by Fairfield as short-termism that failed to consider the long view of the industrial company, but for scores of people critical to GE’s fate, the stock price was ultimately their money.

It was one thing to abide by the principles of performance-based stock compensation when the company could take a hit over a quarter or two, get its stride back, and rise again, bringing the price back up to a range where the options that executives had earned would be worth more than they cost to cash in (the deal tacitly on offer when the options were priced in the first place). GE’s inability to climb back to the multiples it had once enjoyed didn’t just incite grumbling from former board members and retired executives whose options were dying unexercised. It threatened to have a major impact on the recruitment and retention of the best prospects.

Still worse for Immelt, the apparent reclassification of the company by Wall Street hinted at a more dire judgment. It wasn’t just that investors, in assessing their risk of losing money, saw GE as more akin to a major bank than a major manufacturer. The fact that investors made that judgment was a signal of their unwillingness to believe the company’s near-daily protestations that it was an industrial company that happened to have built-in financial advantages. GE’s investment proposition demanded faith in its ability to keep financial risks as firmly in check as the risks of failure in any of its industrial lines. Any jet engine maker would take a hit if a turbine blade broke loose and spun free from a plane in flight. A nuclear reactor meltdown would ding the stock of its manufacturer. An MRI magnet recall would incur a big charge to earnings for a healthcare equipment maker. The premise of GE’s every communication to investors was that its management was so strong, and its vision of the entire conglomerate so penetrating and expansive, that its stock was a gamble worth taking. Even in the inevitable event that something somewhere went wrong, its protocols for working out of trouble would limit the amount of long-term damage and preserve its investors’ money.

The security in a smaller, more stable GE Capital that Immelt now promised was that the financial services arm would be managed in just the same way: the risks it posed to a GE shareholder would not be significantly different from those posed by, say, the century-old locomotive division.

If Wall Street had given the postcrisis GE the industrial multiple that Immelt wanted, it would have meant that investors trusted GE to manage all the arms of its conglomerate to the same degree of risk and security. But Wall Street in 2013 wasn’t offering that kind of multiple because, to some extent, investors just didn’t quite believe what Immelt was promising.



In late 2013, Immelt wasn’t yet publicly using the term that would become shorthand for the maneuver he decided on to get GE out of its middling funk. Like a bulky forward under a basketball hoop who finds himself face to face with a double team threatening a trap, Immelt needed a way to change in two directions at once, to swing away from what wasn’t working and then lunge toward what might.

He needed to pivot.

Even though the tactic he chose had failed him many times before, Immelt decided to essentially double down on it. He wanted to do a giant industrial acquisition that would ramp up the money GE earned from machines and services, opening a new stream of cash into the corporate treasury. That cash, and those earnings, could then be used to make an even bigger move that wasn’t even discussed yet outside of the small groups that regularly huddled at headquarters in Fairfield and GE Capital’s nerve center down the road in Norwalk. If the industrial deal worked, GE would have the financial cushion to move even further to wean itself off financial services. Instead of simply promising a smaller and more secure GE Capital, GE could make a fundamental shift away from the dependence on finance that had defined it for a generation.

But first, the Jeffs—Immelt and Bornstein—and John Flannery, the head of corporate M&A, needed a suitably big target. They needed a whale of an industrial deal, something as large as the acquisitions of Amersham and NBC Universal had been. But unlike those deals, they needed one that Wall Street would actually like.

So word went out from Fairfield to Atlanta and Chicago and Schenectady, to Cincinnati and London: send us the biggest targets in your sights.

Adam Smith and the Power team immediately thought of just such a large target—not that they thought much of the property. Still, a call for pitches was a call for pitches, and there could be much value for GE’s vaunted cost-cutting machine to reap from a declining French conglomerate with assets strewn across three continents, provided they could snap it up at a buyer’s price. The Power and Water division’s business development team dropped their idea into the stream of proposals now flowing toward Connecticut.

What if GE bought Alstom?





26


New Lieutenant





YEARS OF FITFUL progress on reorienting GE’s industrial business lines toward areas where the global economy might soon begin to grow again were doing little for the all-important stock price. Even as Immelt and his advisers privately deliberated over what big acquisitions or sales would rapidly swing the public’s idea of GE’s core business and strengths, the market refused to deviate from the clear signal it had flashed on and off since the financial crisis. A certain type of investor would never trust GE as long as it relied so heavily on finance to make its numbers. Finding a way out of Capital was the only way to get rid of the stock market funk that bedeviled Immelt.

He started with partial measures, not going cold turkey, but beginning to wean the company off the easy money it had grown so accustomed to. By 2013, Immelt’s public forecasts about the future shape of the company were crystalizing into a vision that investors could see as well. GE would shrink its reliance on Capital while also shrinking Capital itself. Units like the consumer finance business, which backed store-brand credit cards and payment plans, would be shed and Capital’s total size, measured by the value of its assets, would be allowed to shrink. GE still needed GE Capital, and assured investors that it was perfectly compatible with the other industrial units, but acknowledged that letting it grow to the equivalent of the seventh-largest bank in America was letting its heft get out of hand. Simultaneously, he said, GE could increase revenue and profitability on the industrial side of the ledger enough to push GE’s earnings toward a 70/30 split: 70 percent from the industrial side of the house, and 30 percent from finance. Then Capital could resume growing, after five years of sporadic recovery from its near-death experience in the crisis.

But this time, Immelt promised, GE would grow its financial service arm “in line” with its manufacturing businesses. GE would never get as hooked on the easy profits of lending money as it had been before the crisis hit, he reassured investors, but the company could still be run so well that it would glean all the benefits of its finance days, especially the ability to consistently nail short-term earnings targets and please the market.

Immelt was issuing a tall order to the executives who sat just beneath him in the corporate hierarchy. To make good on these promises, Immelt would have to rearrange the very highest levels of his management team.

As GE’s chief financial officer, Keith Sherin had long served as a counterweight to Immelt’s flights of optimistic reasoning. A tall man with the tightly cropped hair of a state trooper, Sherin rode a Harley-Davidson in the rolling hills of western Connecticut on weekends. He answered questions on conference calls in a dry drone that sometimes failed to contain his annoyance, and could be relied upon at times to pull Immelt back from where his impulses had taken him. Sherin had not been influential enough to save Immelt from creating a record of notorious overpayment in mergers and acquisitions, but he was generally viewed as a force that made Immelt consider sober realities when the CEO, the consummate salesman, could think of little but the upside.

Now Sherin’s role was changing. He would take over GE Capital, becoming its CEO as the company tried to deliver on the unrealistic promise to Wall Street (which Wall Street had, in effect, demanded) to make GE Capital less crucial to GE’s fate while retaining all its powers to goose the quarterly numbers. Sherin would oversee the early efforts to shed some of Capital’s units and reduce the financial arm’s overall size. He would also be the veteran GE leader who oversaw GE’s supervision by the Federal Reserve, the discipline imposed on the company by the federal laws passed in the wake of the crisis.

In Sherin’s place at Immelt’s right hand in Fairfield would be Jeff Bornstein, the blunt former CFO of GE Capital who had helped the lending businesses—and thus the company itself—weather the worst of the financial crisis and stave off insolvency. Bornstein, like Sherin, cultivated an image of fearless bluntness. He also lacked the baggage of long service as the CFO of a company whose finances remained relatively opaque and so had a certain amount of credibility with stock market analysts. They liked his unfiltered style, at least when he wasn’t barking at them. Immelt viewed the job in part as a reward for Bornstein’s critical work staving off the financial panic. “You can tell a lot about somebody when they’re in the shitstorm,” Immelt said.

If Sherin’s job was best understood as redefining Capital’s role, both internally and for investors, it would fall to Bornstein to take a gimlet eye to the rest of GE’s sprawling business. Even if Immelt was keeping the terms of GE’s success characteristically hard to pin down, one easy measure of whether his overhaul of the company was working would be whether industrial revenues and earnings rose (or didn’t rise) to meet the challenge of shrinking GE Capital. Bornstein quickly saw that the company’s operations, which increasingly ranged widely around the world, were riddled with clutter, duplication, waste, inefficiency, and complexity. Raised in the GE culture of Welch, with little tolerance for bullshit, he saw pent-up profit that could be gleaned from simply cutting. Releasing the synergies that would come from eliminating redundancies—that is, people GE didn’t need anymore—would lead to higher earnings. As renewed attention led them to try to whip the industrial businesses into shape, Bornstein, Immelt, and the rest of the GE top brass soon settled on a name for the program they embarked on in 2013: Simplification.

The program had begun under Sherin, who worked with some of the company executives overseeing GE’s foreign operations. Together they had worked out ways to cut some costs, for example, on back-office staff and customer financing. Simplification wasn’t just about cost-cutting, however, as Bornstein told stock analysts, and company flacks explained to news reporters. What the company would achieve through its Simplification program was far more than just higher profits accruing from the closure of a few factories or a couple of rounds of layoffs in underperforming verticals.

In a ninety-minute call with investors and analysts that fall, Bornstein promised a deep interrogation of the business practices in GE’s divisions. Foreign operations needed to be closer to their customers, he said, and the duplicate functions in every country that GE had set up were not necessarily needed. He questioned why GE was operating with more than five hundred separate P&Ls (profit and loss statements) for a business. The company used hundreds of different enterprise software systems to manage everything from the flow of parts to service contracts to sales data. That could all be winnowed down and reduced. Its competitors were more willing to use shared-service agreements than GE, whose different business units struck parallel deals for basic industrial services in the same markets.

On that call, Bornstein pointed to new GE facilities like an industrial park in Pune, India, designed from the ground up to be multimodal: in the same building, GE could produce components for a freight locomotive or a giant wind turbine blade, adjusting the assembly line as needed based on the flow of orders. That kind of thinking would save the company millions over time, and it reflected the efforts of its leaders to get away from the classic long-term planning model of their industrial forebears and mimic Silicon Valley instead. A leaner, simplified GE would focus less on the multiyear product development projects that had defined it for generations and try to engineer more quickly to a customer’s whims—and cut its losses more quickly on what wasn’t working.

In an investor meeting in November 2013, Bornstein said that Simplification also required the company to rethink corporate’s role. This was a smart thing to say to investors: they were always eager to hear that money could be saved at the fortresslike Fairfield corporate headquarters, or that capital would be redirected from GE’s traditionally top-heavy leadership operations and out into the businesses where products were made and sold. But Bornstein was also hinting at a GE heresy, the very question that people who wanted to stay on management’s good side didn’t entertain in public: what good was it, really, to continue to operate a complicated conglomerate of tangentially affiliated businesses when that model had proven to be impractical in many ways, was often unmanageable at the highest levels, and sometimes even seemed to be doomed?

“We have got $3 billion of costs at corporate that is not allocated to the businesses,” Bornstein said, “and we are going through our own re-imagining work now as a team in Fairfield, thinking about: What is the role of corporate? How do we add value to the businesses? What are those things we do that enhance their competitiveness? What are those things we do that don’t add a ton of value to those businesses?” GE executives were now “rethinking every dollar we spend in Fairfield,” he told investors, and would continue to do so for years.

Analysts liked this kind of thing. They liked Simplification, and what it meant that GE was asking itself these questions. Just the same, some of them realized that shaking up the structure—including by cutting out the lower-level P&Ls and shedding fat in some of the far-flung industrial markets—would change the way managerial talent moved up and through GE. The company believed—as an article of unchallenged faith—that the way smart men and women graduated from one assignment to the next at GE had taught them how to become great managers.

So when Bornstein said that, in far-flung markets with revenues in the mere hundreds of millions, “you don’t need a complete functional team and the infrastructure that comes with managing and reporting a P&L at that level,” an analyst named Steve Winoker replied, “That was how you raised managers, though.”

The company would fall back on what had worked at GE Capital, Bornstein countered. Instead of a single executive overseeing basic business functions at these smaller operations, a shared back office would handle the nuts and bolts of payroll, billing, hiring, and so on. “The way we think about it is, running a business is not about being responsible for accounts payable and payroll,” Bornstein said. “It is about owning customers and markets.”

All GE executives should be focused on one central, critical job: selling.





27


Playing Startup





ERIC RIES LOOKED the part. Black-framed eyeglasses, modestly untamed hair, casual dress, and an expansive way of gesticulating as he made his points about how the company of the future would run. Ries was the author of a runaway business book hit, The Lean Startup, which professed to explain why the companies that had best captured the public imagination in the second decade of the 2000s had succeeded and how bigger, slower, older companies might emulate them to succeed as well. Ries parlayed the success of his book into speaking engagements and conference appearances, did consulting, wrote more books, and proselytized “lean principles” to the leaders of big corporations. Through Beth Comstock, Eric Ries came to the attention of Jeff Immelt.

The lean startup methodology borrowed in part from lean manufacturing, which GE and other big American manufacturers had already begun to rely upon. Lean manufacturing slimmed down the traditional business model of maintaining inventory warehouses and stocking up factories with supply chain parts in favor of a constantly running demand engine that took in pieces and churned out assembled machines “just in time”—that is, as they were needed. Ries applied the lean principles that executives of Immelt’s age had been using to wring new levels of efficiency out of manufacturing companies since the 1980s to the venture capital–funded startups that dotted the economic landscape of the 2010s. Ries argued that new firms as well as more established companies needed to act like the most successful software startups. They should design fluid responses to consumer demand, iterate and reiterate product designs, and be unafraid to go to market with what wasn’t yet perfect or to walk away from what had been judged a failure.

This was, in short, not the way GE worked. Designers and engineers in the appliance business planned months in advance, developing diverse lines of products to hit every market niche, in anticipation several seasons ahead of retailers’ holiday sales. Even less accustomed to such a freewheeling operation were the Power, Aviation, and Healthcare businesses. Their products were incredibly complex machines whose success or failure was measured in tiny increments and by thousands of hours of service without a breakdown. The stakes of an industrial update could not be higher: unlike a software patch pushed out before its time, the consequences of failure could be dire, up to and including death.

Still, executives like Comstock, Immelt, and some of their colleagues were convinced that such arguments arose from the fading throes of resistance to an inevitable adaptation. GE wouldn’t pump out jet engines that would seize up like bad apps midflight. But the physical, manufactured world appeared to be experiencing a sea change not so far removed from what was already under way in the economy of software and cloud computing. The promise of 3-D printing—or “additive manufacturing” in GE’s wonkier preferred phrasing—shimmered like a mirage in a future not that far off. A radically new method of engineering metal parts could solve problems of weight and efficiency that had been intractable for decades, especially in the all-important design of jet engines.

The technology was far from perfected, but GE was starting to experiment—for example, by shaping the first rough parts of its products entirely on additive printing bays, then having human workers finish them by hand. The printers now could produce a jet engine fuel nozzle that had previously been made of twenty parts out of a single piece of metal that was significantly lighter. It did feel as if a higher-tech industry’s innovations were beginning to manifest all around the modern industrial world, and Immelt was convinced—not only at the urging of self-styled thinkers like Ries but also by his own instinct to make heady bets—that GE’s path forward should be to surge ahead as a leader in technology. The strategy would follow the story line that the company would tell. GE imagined itself a leader in innovation. And so innovate it would.

And Immelt used it to distinguish himself from the management mumbo jumbo and financial engineering that so many associated with GE, saying in 2016 that “additive manufacturing makes a shitload more sense than Six Sigma did.”

The hope was also that embracing the language of innovation would prove to be a self-fulfilling promise. If GE talked like one of the buzzy tech unicorns dominating the business press and seemingly taking over the nation’s economy, maybe it in turn would also be covered like one. Immelt was frustrated, he acknowledged in occasional asides to GE executives and board members, at how intractable the narrative about his company and himself had become. He and his top aides could point to any number of exogenous shocks to explain some of the company’s sluggish performance under his tenure: the attacks of September 11 and the ensuing recession; the financial meltdown and the years of economic uncertainty it wrought; or the political distractions of the postcrisis years, including the rise of an isolationist and anti-establishment American right wing that had increasingly hamstrung GE’s symbiotic and profitable relationship with the federal government.

But ultimately, though Immelt’s flacks claimed that he never considered it, it all came down to the price of a General Electric share. Although it was widely acknowledged that GE stock was never going back to what it had been under Welch at the peak, certainly no one was happy that it remained so middling. The recently retired executives and directors muttered about their underwater stock options. The business TV commentators invoked it in the never-ending comparison contest between Jack and Jeff—a contest Immelt was always losing. While Immelt adhered to his GE training to fixate on shareholder return and the quick beats that would drive shares higher, he and others in the company wondered in frustration why the world couldn’t just focus, for a minute, on something else. There was so much to brag about in the company—the sheer grandeur and force of the things it made, the legacy (however much it had to be spun) that stretched back to the most famous of American inventors, the way the company remained deeply embedded in American communities, still employing hundreds of thousands of people not only around the world but across the nation.

In spotlighting these impressive features of the company, Immelt and the GE message machine wove strands of rhetoric that imbued its underlying businesses with a greater purpose, an association with genius. The turbines and engines and the factories in which they were made were not just impressive, but “brilliant.” The company’s business strength lay not so much in the designing, building, and selling of intricate machines, but in the feats of imagination that conjured them into being. Her years in the entertainment business had taught Comstock to always return to themes of inquiry and adventure—to keep the mission focused on associating the familiar GE meatball logo with forays into the unknown, with discovery. A BBDO advertisement, for instance, featured an ungainly-looking feathered beast being coaxed into revealing its plumage: a representation of nothing less than GE’s unique ability to cultivate The Idea itself.

GE aggressively pushed the business press to cover it in the same optimistic tone and to suspend disbelief occasionally in the same way it did for companies from the purported new economy. GE pushed the story of itself as a company every bit as innovative as Tesla Motors, as attuned to changing consumer attitudes as Apple, not just in traditional news outlets and on cable channels but in the flourishing ranks of new social media–directed news and analysis platforms, like BuzzFeed, Instagram, and Bloomberg’s TicToc—journalism as short and shallow as a post or a tweet.

The daily deluge of upbeat, speculative granules about technological breakthroughs—like the musings by Tesla CEO Elon Musk about high-speed travel by “hyperloop”—lacked or downplayed any of the inconvenient context of a traditional news report, particularly caveats about the obstacles between the present and the unicorns’ rosy visions of the future. GE and Jeff Immelt wanted in on that. Their language was changing too. Like the ride-hailing company Lyft essentially “inventing” the concept of the bus, GE was no longer content to boast about running efficiently, or well, or even better than any other company, but only about changing everything. Its system for performance reviews, the structure of its annual shareholder report, its approach to digital technology—in all these areas and others, GE announced itself as revolutionary. The company said it operated just like the freewheeling venture capital–funded companies churning down from Sand Hill Road; leading with slick design and the promise of widespread adoption, GE’s PR, like the startups,’ was less focused on how its business units were going to turn regular, dependable profits.

With its obliging corporate messaging engine revved up, GE went into the content business. The company drones swooped through turbine halls, capturing Instagram footage of its brilliant machines. In Washington, the company lured reporters into demonstration booths to learn about how FastWorks, its implementation of the gospel of Eric Ries, would upend the design of home appliances, and how GE, partnering with Local Motors, would reinvent the business of making cars through 3-D printing. To celebrate the forty-fifth anniversary of the Apollo 11 moon landing, GE’s PR team paired with a shoemaker and website called JackThreads to make a sneaker based on the astronauts’ moon boots, a reference to the fact that a type of silicone used in the boots worn on the mission had been designed by GE. The company finagled a photo of Buzz Aldrin wearing the shoes and arranged write-ups in places like Fashionista, Adweek, and The Verge, outlets that a previous generation of corporate executives wouldn’t have recognized, much less cared about.

By March of 2016, after GE had spent years pressing the case, Bloomberg Businessweek obliged with the headline of Jeff Immelt’s dreams: “How GE Exorcised the Ghost of Jack Welch to Become a 124-Year-Old Startup.”





28


Cowboys into Farmers





AS GE COURTED new media attention and sought the public adoration it so envied in the startup economy of Silicon Valley, real life didn’t get any easier. Not with the Federal Reserve sitting in the offices of GE Capital.

No one had ever had to worry about bank supervision in the good old days of GE Capital. Back then, the business had proudly run on something like an honor code. Sales teams chased deals, won approval on the strength of their pitches to higher-ups, and either managed to make money for the company or suffered the results of an investment gone south, such as a docked bonus or a missed promotion.

But this model was anathema to the postcrisis New York Fed. Where had the company actually written down, the bank supervisors wanted to know, the absolute criteria for extending a line of credit? (They hadn’t.) Where was the risk analysis of the loans on the Capital balance sheet? Why did it have such rosy projections about which customers would pay their money back? (Capital was supremely confident in its ability to lend and foreclose without losses like the banks had suffered.) And what the hell had GE Capital been doing buying a Brazilian oil-drilling rig? (Long story.)

The teams demanding answers to these questions in the spring of 2013 reported to Caroline Frawley, the head of a New York Fed unit that had been set loose to pore through GE Capital’s books after the post–financial crisis Dodd-Frank reforms were enacted. This scrutiny sounded reasonable enough to majorities in Congress, who, having witnessed GE’s near-collapse in the crisis, thought it made sense to have the same regulators who were keeping a closer eye on the assets and balance sheets of big banks also looking at the same information from GE Capital. Capital, in the months leading up to the crash, had been effectively the seventh-largest bank in the country, but its operations had been almost impenetrable. It was a “black box,” everyone had said with a shrug—until the black box had almost dragged the company down for good.

Bank regulators and GE Capital’s proud core of deal-makers didn’t see balance sheets in the same way. And they really didn’t like each other. What Capital viewed as flexibility, and a culture of savvy deal-making, professional bank supervisors were apt to see as the absence of any meaningful rules at all. And GE Capital’s books were, to those experienced with the protocols of modern banks, a riot of tangential and unmatched risks. GE Capital’s deals—some of them entered into only to aid the industrial business units, and many of them just speculative bets—were so wide-ranging and hard to value that the exchanges between the Fed teams and Capital executives often turned combative. Capital had purchased the oil-drilling rig as part of a bet on an increase in super-deep oil exploration off the coast of Brazil (a bet the broader oil market would soon turn against). It was one of the curious assets on the Capital books that had gone south, to the surprise of the Fed team. The legal entity that formally owned the Peregrine I, a “dynamically positioned drilling vehicle,” had filed for bankruptcy two years earlier.

The rigorous protocols of the banking industry also bore little resemblance to the customs that prevailed at GE Capital. Like the parent company, GE Capital’s system for evaluating and monitoring deals was rooted in personal accountability more than enterprise risk.

A GE compliance official hired to help deal with the incoming attention from the Fed supervisors asked what Capital’s standard protocol for extending a new line of credit required. What chain of command signed off on the deal? What committee reviewed it? These were questions that any bank would have answered easily. But at Capital, the new official eventually learned, there was no concrete protocol. The guy who brought in the deal was responsible for it, and his bonus was on the line if the loan didn’t work. There was no hard and fast rule, however, about how basic issuances of credit should go. GE defended this model as the essence of executive accountability. And investors were getting what they always said they wanted: deal-makers’ pay being exposed to their success in achieving the goals of the business.

But to Fed supervisors, this was a recipe for sending a system out of control. GE’s protocol, or lack of it, wasn’t just unusual practice. It was dangerous, both to the company and to the very system the Fed was trying to protect.



“I am not going to let that woman tell me how to run my company.”

Jeff Immelt’s voice was compressed by anger into a hiss. It was 2014, and Immelt and GE’s leadership were boiling over with resentment about one woman in particular: Frawley, whose team of supervisors from the Federal Reserve were clambering all over GE Capital and its inner workings, demanding reams of information about the company’s deals, procedures, and debts.

Even worse, for Immelt, was Frawley’s insistence on using every power the law provided her. She was a hard cop who followed the letter of every statute and regulation. That included Fed officials being able to sit in on meetings of GE’s board of directors and listen to discussions about the company’s financial exposure. The Fed supervisors were also meeting with the company’s directors outside the confines of board meetings, without Immelt or his lieutenants present, and sometimes one-on-one with the directors, urging each one to take a harder line with Immelt. They felt that directors needed to question him more aggressively about the amount of risk that remained on GE’s books half a decade after the company had almost collapsed under Capital’s weight.

For a CEO accustomed to dictating the content and tenor of his board’s deliberations, this felt like intrusive meddling, if not an outright assault on the company. It was also costing GE a hell of a lot of money. Privately GE executives threw around the unprovable estimate of $1 billion a year in new costs to meet the compliance requirements dictated to them by the Fed’s bank examiner teams. What had once been a team of near-autonomous deal-makers who thought of themselves as cowboys more than farmers, in one executive’s turn of phrase, was now a fenced-in operation. The vast portfolios of one-offs, “reasonable expansions” into new markets, and partner plays—Capital financing exotic projects far afield to loosen the soil for some of GE’s industrial businesses to sow the seeds of future sales—were all now constantly scrutinized by the Fed.

GE Capital had always considered the quirky diversity of its books its strength. Certainly in the “mid market,” where the company had long thrived, GE deal-makers felt that they were deeply familiar with niches that banks ignored and that, thanks to the corporate parent’s massive balance sheet, they could compete by the narrowest margins to win business. Hence the outstanding loans to snowmobile makers in South Dakota and potato chip makers in Ohio. Capital had armies of deal-makers who were not so much bankers as door-to-door salesmen hawking money. In those mid-market swaths of the company, there were few MBAs who boasted a Harvard pedigree and wore blue suits.

“Our folks come across to people as GE people,” a Capital executive told one of us in 2015. “We don’t wear suspenders. We don’t have MBAs, for the most part. We don’t come across as sort of a Wall Street, big bank kind of culture.”

What they did have was deep knowledge of the assets that backed the loans they were writing and a better track record than the big banks at completing what Capital folks called “workouts”—foreclosing and seizing assets when the borrowers couldn’t pay. Capital prided itself on knowing the market for the railroad tank cars that it accepted as collateral, or for the manure-spreaders that backed loans to farmers in the Midwest.

True, the unit had gone crazy for leveraged buyouts in the 1980s and ’90s, only to see many of the targeted businesses go belly up in the recession. As one former GE Capital executive quipped, that had been an experiment in private equity by accident. But they had managed to keep businesses standing, turned them profitable, and finally resold them, sometimes years later. GE Capital people were sure of themselves, even as their operations got larger and more complex and broadened into areas of finance where the bigger players played and the risks were potentially catastrophic—as in their growing portfolios of real estate and the lingering remains of the insurance business. Capital people, imbued with tribal pride in their unit, insisted that they knew what they were doing.

The Fed treated them like idiots. Or at least that’s what it felt like to many of GE’s executives, Immelt included. Some of his directors felt the same way. One marveled in 2014 at the tone of the written exchanges between Frawley and her team and Keith Sherin and his top deputies, which the audit committee reviewed in its role as oversight over Capital. Why, the director asked, did the Fed team always have to sound so mean?

But Frawley’s skepticism of GE’s internal controls wasn’t undeserved. Even now, years past the crisis, Capital remained a black box even to those who were paid to understand the company intimately, like sell-side stock market analysts and even some GE executives. The company’s remaining risks were unfathomably deep, some prescient investors felt. And the hubris that characterized decision-making elsewhere in the company could be just as prevalent at Capital.

One former GE executive recalled attending a presentation at Crotonville just months after the company had barely staved off disaster in the depths of the crisis. One of the speakers that day was Mike Neal, still the CEO of GE Capital. Neal embodied GE Capital. He had captained the second half of its gargantuan expansion in size and importance to the company, warmly rallying his troops in his thick Georgia drawl. He stood out like a blue collar in a business full of white ones, one of his admirers said. Under Neal, Capital had somehow avoided disaster and managed to close the books on 2009 in the black, a fact that the company would brag about for years afterward.

Neal’s presentation that day on what GE had learned from its near-death experience amounted to a fatalistic shrug. He showed no outward sign of self-examination or contrition about the risks the financial unit had taken on, the dangerous excesses it had driven in a quest for earnings, or the way Capital had gambled the company, knowingly or not, on a roulette wheel of short-term commercial paper and nearly lost. Neal’s analysis was macro. He spoke about what had bedeviled the larger financial system and the signs in the market they’d have to do a better job of reading next time, without acknowledging that GE should never have been in certain markets in the first place. GE had been hit, Neal seemed to be saying, by the unforeseeable, by a one-hundred-year perfect storm. And they had weathered it.

The former executive listening to Neal’s version of events in Crotonville that day squirmed in his seat. My God, he thought to himself. You have learned nothing.





29


Project Hubble





AS THE SPRING of 2013 unfolded, Mike Neal had waited as usual for his annual consultation with Immelt. Although the documents on file at HR might have suggested otherwise, the business unit CEOs served at the pleasure of the boss, and Immelt’s ability to nudge them aside was always unquestioned. As it turned out, Neal was not allowed to stick around to see what would happen to his beloved GE Capital.

Big changes were coming for Capital. The market would not yield to Immelt’s desire for a revaluation. As long as Wall Street believed that America’s great industrial company was in fact a massive bank with a couple of turbine businesses bolted on the sides, nothing about Jeff Immelt’s reputation or the company’s stock price would change meaningfully.

It wasn’t just the stock multiple’s resistance to change that bedeviled GE Capital. The unit was discovering that life was different in the aftermath of the crisis, and not just because Caroline Frawley’s team was patrolling the halls and listening in on board meetings. The essential element of GE Capital, from the Welch revolution on, had been the cost of money. They had borrowed low, thanks to GE’s sterling credit rating, and lent at aggressive rates, undercutting banks for narrow bands of customers that their competitors didn’t fully appreciate. Whereas banks with hoards of depositors’ cash could always find the lowest cost of capital to lend out—nothing was cheaper than a dollar already in hand—using its huge stores of commercial paper had enabled GE to pick its spots and find deals, sometimes holding on to more of its booked loans than a bank would. The crisis had upended all that.

Now, in the aftermath, GE’s reliance on commercial paper to fund its deals and lending had become untenable. And at the same time the retrenched banks, flush with the greater stores of deposits and capital that regulators insisted they hold on to, were chasing the deals that GE Capital had once had the lead on. “There’s just so much money now chasing new business,” the Capital executive said. To chase those deals in turn, GE was squeezing its profit margins.

In one sense, this was just the story of finance in the postcrisis period: figuring what could and couldn’t make money in the new regime of rules put in place after an orgy of profit madness had nearly crushed the global financial system. But the wrinkle in the story at General Electric was the new relevance of a question that not even the near-destruction of the company had prompted, but that seemed germane now that GE Capital’s margins were flagging and its usefulness in managing corporate earnings was waning: was GE Capital really worth the trouble anymore?



A newly hired General Electric executive was waiting by the gatehouse at the entrance to the corporate campus in Fairfield one morning in 2013. Suddenly, one of the guards looked up at a colleague in a panic and began shouting, “Gate! Gate!” The second man leaped forward and landed hand-first on the button that opened an entrance gate just in time to lift the bar out of the way of a car that roared past up the drive, never braking. The new hire thought it looked like a late-model Porsche. Behind the wheel, unmistakably, was Jeff Bornstein.

Bornstein was a hard-charging guy, short with a close-shaven, balding head and the presence of an enforcer. The GE press staff confirmed on background that it was nicotine gum he was so often chomping on when he made the rounds, glad-handing the investors, parrying the sell-side guys, gracing the reporters with an off-the-record quip or two. They also confirmed that he was a shark fisherman, though Bornstein himself would clarify that it was tuna he hunted. Despite his reputation, Bornstein was charming. He could be condescending and even harsh when he dressed down subordinates, but he had a warm cackle and disarming candor that many in the company’s upper echelons lacked, especially his predecessor, the automatonic Keith Sherin.

Early in Bornstein’s tenure as CFO, one GE director remarked that he wasn’t the GE type, but in a good way. This person meant that Bornstein spoke candidly about things that had to change and was unaffected by the nostalgia for GE’s traditions and long-standing business lines.

Having reached the job of CFO, Bornstein now sat at the apex of an obscure internal hierarchy of financial operatives who were interspersed through the company’s scores of companies and divisions.



By early 2014, the meetings had begun—always in secret, always in small groups. These informal ad hoc gatherings—which included Bornstein, Denniston, Immelt, Sherin, and a handful of others—were considering the unthinkable.

What if they could find a way out of GE Capital?

Such thinking had been bolstered by the success of spinning off part of GE’s consumer finance business as Synchrony in 2014. The first plan explored by the company’s teams, dubbed Project Beacon, was the possibility of slicing GE Capital into a separate independent company. This analysis showed that the move would entail massive tax costs and other risks. A second approach was developed to simply sell off the major pieces of the division; this alternative, dubbed Project Hubble, would still have large tax costs and bring its own uncertainties. The final decision would come later, but for now GE was heading in the direction of Hubble.

The complications were enormous. First, of course, they needed to find buyers for huge, diverse assets in the GE Capital balance sheet—office buildings and corporate parks, rail tank car fleets, rafts of loans to fast-food franchisees and farmers. They would need to resolve the taxes, including the disposition of billions in deferred losses, which now were accounted as tax assets that GE could use to boost its earnings. Above all there was the consideration of earnings. The successful sale of GE Capital’s parts would generate big onetime gains, the GE executives thought, especially if they were all sold off quickly. Those gains could be reinvested, either in industrial businesses or, preferably, in stock buybacks, which would mop up outstanding shares and boost the price.

But GE would also need to prepare for a vastly different future in which the parent company would be operating without the reliable, regular dividend of earnings flowing up from Capital into the corporate treasury. Could GE reliably hit its earnings targets without the tools it had used at corporate to smooth out rough quarters and boost its paper profits when needed?

The leaders of the company were quietly considering a kind of disarmament. They wanted to see if they could finally shed their tremendous, but troublesome, financial weapon by laying it down entirely. They considered the question with the characteristic self-assurance of GE men. Even without the use of Capital—which some likened to a steroid that boosted the company’s performance artificially—they were certain that they could generate huge profits, pay the unusually rich dividend that kept shareholders happy, and squeeze greater profit out of the aging manufacturing base of jet engines, power turbines, and MRI machines. They would keep going global—expanding abroad into new markets in search of the growing international middle class whose demands for a higher standard of living underpinned GE’s commercial strategy. They would gobble up market share in everything from power turbines to medical devices. They would be rewarded by Wall Street for moving on from finance. They would tell the world a story about innovation and excellence, and they would be believed. As the spring of 2015 arrived, it was clear that GE was going with Project Hubble, which was still a secret kept from everyone except the highest echelon of GE executives and a small team of its most trusted bankers, including Jimmy Lee.

GE was getting ready to sell Capital.





30


The Chop Shop





A SERIES OF PHONE calls and texts had the GE communications shop worried. It was April 2015, and they were hours away from unveiling the fruits of Project Hubble, a multifaceted deal to unload a huge portfolio of real estate and mortgage assets to Blackstone. In effectively exiting the troublesome real estate business, GE was announcing the broad outlines of what would come to be known as the “Capital exit plan.”

Nearly all of GE Capital was going up for sale. Placing a premium on speed, the company would offer to sell its lending operations in chunks. Big banks and private equity funds would snap up the obscure pieces of the lending operation, taking over loans to small businesses, machine middlemen, and leveraged buyout shops. The plan would call for GE to hang on to GECAS, the jewel in Capital’s crown; that unit owned and leased a massive fleet of planes and jet engines, reaping profits with every launch of a commercial airliner into the air. It would also hang on to the stubs that facilitated the operations of the industrial businesses, such as those that lent for the development of new power installations or financed the purchase of healthcare equipment.

GE would also be left with what amounted to the unwanted detritus in the basement of GE Capital—the odds and ends of deals gone sideways or bets that hadn’t panned out over the decades of aggressively pursuing returns with borrowed funds. (To the surprise of some observers, GE’s investor slides would include a graphic showing a sliver of “insurance” among the assets that would be left behind in the Capital exit. Insurance was a business GE was supposed to have left behind a decade earlier.)

If top management pulled it off, the announcement would put a charge into GE’s stock, sending it soaring on hopes that the lumbering corporation was still capable of moving quickly and shedding the dependence on lending that its most critical investors simply hadn’t trusted.

Like most major GE news, the announcement was being carefully stage-managed. The news would break simultaneously on CNBC and in the New York Times, which had been supplied with the news under an embargo. But now reporters from the Wall Street Journal were sniffing around, sensing a major deal afoot. Reporters who covered real estate had gotten wind of a large and mysterious transaction under way. Colleagues on the deals team were bombarding banker sources, trying to gauge the scale and price of what was on the block. Corporate beat reporters called sources of their own, grilling GE executives and members of the board about what the company was up to and asking why the deal was shrouded in an unusual level of secrecy.

Company flacks called reporters at the Wall Street Journal to offer a major story if the paper would agree to an embargo. Already close to a scoop, the reporters and editors conferred and decided, with the competition tightening, to decline.

The first piece of the news appeared in a 145-word story on the Dow Jones Newswires in the early afternoon of April 9. “General Electric Co. is close to selling all or part of its giant real estate holdings, people familiar with the matter said, as the conglomerate moves more aggressively to shrink its finance business,” the Journal reported, leading with the news of one of the largest property deals ever as its reporters raced to confirm what else was coming. GE’s stock began to rise that afternoon as investors seized on the sign that Immelt might at last be ready to make a serious move away from finance. The real estate move alone would have been a huge story, but it was clear to reporters and editors in New York that something much larger was afoot as well. An all-nighter ensued as reporters called and texted tight-lipped sources who were sworn to secrecy, pleading and badgering for information about the deal.

By the early morning hours of April 10, the boyish and inexhaustible corporate editor for the Wall Street Journal, Drew Dowell, was at home in New Jersey, conferring with reporters as his finger hovered over his computer mouse, ready to hit Send. A key source had come through for veteran Journal reporter Joann S. Lublin, a newsroom fixture for more than four decades whose tenacity in wearing down corporate titans who dared to resist her inquiries about hirings, firings, coups, and deals was legendary.

Dowell weighed the sourcing, the strength of the confirmations, the gravity of the news the Journal was about to report, and the fierce urge to beat the competition—especially those he could be sure had the whole story straight from the company, tied up in a bow and waiting for the appointed hour to publish. He hit Send.

The Journal headlines hit the wire at 6:25 A.M., five minutes before the GE press release: “GE Prepared to Exit the Bulk of GE Capital—Sources.”

GE’s announcement landed with the bang that Immelt, Bornstein, Sherin, and the board had hoped. The stock shot upward, and commentators praised GE’s move. The news coverage noted that Immelt was putting his own unmistakable stamp on the company, utterly altering the vehicle he’d been bequeathed by Welch. And there was more good news to come, the company said.

In the public announcement of the exit plan, GE said that the asset sales would be completed over twenty-four months, obviously setting a target for GE’s executives to hit and surpass. The focus, the company insisted, would be on divesting these assets quickly. The logic behind that strategy was twofold. First, a drawn-out period of separation could depress morale and distract from normal business. Indeed, even the relatively speedy process that ensued had its awkward moments. For instance, a temporary wall was erected to separate one of the units slated to be sold but not yet disposed of from the rest of the Capital office suite in Norwalk, Connecticut.

Second, many of the business units were really only as valuable as their people. Holding the value of Antares, the internal unit that provided financing for middle-market private equity deal-makers, required selling it off intact, and fast, before its leading executives could be recruited away by competitors. And those competitors were already circling, their interest especially directed at poaching Capital executives from units whose ultimate destinations were unclear.

Suddenly executives had little prospect of a career-long stay at GE unless they found a new perch back in the parent company. And there was no security in waiting for their business unit to be swallowed up by a large bank or fund, which would have its own arcane system of seniority and command as well as the classic tool for ensuring the profitability of a new acquisition: laying off redundant employees.

So GE promised swift execution of its sales, and Sherin began whipping sell teams to get buyers interested, set up data rooms, and begin the hard work of valuing Capital’s often amorphous P&Ls for the arriving teams of bankers.

Some found the timing curious. Was GE selling at the right time to get the most value for these businesses it had built over decades? GE didn’t care—it wanted out. It saw the opportunity and needed to take it. Otherwise, as Immelt told people, they ran the risk of another wave of contagion spreading into the financial markets if they waited for something better. That could be the end of GE. It was better now to sell on their terms.

GE said that its decision to sell off Capital would focus and energize the already promised reorientation to its industrial operations, which would provide 90 percent of GE’s earnings by 2018, the company said in its announcement. It also admitted that the change wouldn’t come cheap. GE would take an after-tax charge of $16 billion, mostly noncash, to account for the cost of unwinding Capital’s complex tax schemes and repatriating the billions in cash it held in operations overseas. But the company would shower its existing investor base in profits from the deals. The Capital asset sales were projected to yield $35 billion in cash to the parent company, the press release noted, adding that the board had authorized a new round of stock buybacks, up to $50 billion.

GE’s move was extremely self-assured. It projected the image of a company that had aggressively used its financial wing for a generation to manage its earnings and now was telling the world, backed by its Wall Street reputation, that it could shed that piece of itself and not just fail to suffer but actually perform better than it had in a decade.

Some executives, even some within GE Capital, worried about GE cutting off a source of high-quality earnings: payments on leases, loans, and buildings came in hard cash and flowed up through the internal dividend to the corporate office. GE had plenty of earnings, but many of them didn’t represent cash payments into the company coffers. When the service businesses stretched, they created profits on paper, and so the quarterly search for actual cash could grow desperate in the company’s lean times. Turning off a reliable cash source, these executives thought, was not something to be done cavalierly. As one former senior executive put it, GE would be naked to the world. But it wasn’t in the company’s DNA to doubt itself. Such self-questioning was akin to disloyalty in the eyes of leaders like Immelt.

One morning at the GE Capital headquarters off the Merritt Parkway in Norwalk, Sherin walked into an office where several executives who reported to him were gathered. The exit plan was still a secret, but top-level officials at Capital were beginning to be brought in on the plan.

“We’re going to de-SIFI,” the GE Capital CEO said. That is, GE was planning to shed the Systemically Important Financial Institution categorization that had brought it under the scrutiny of the Fed by selling off most of its finance operation. SIFI status was the regulatory scarlet letter that followed the financial crisis: the designation was applied to the largest banks and financial institutions, those whose failure could trigger another meltdown. The only way to lose the label was to shrink.

“What are we going to do about the cash?” one executive in the room said, wondering how the company would pay its dividend or make the massive investments that the industrial businesses required.

Sherin shrugged. “It’s just work,” he said, repeating a phrase that would become a refrain in the following months filled with single-minded focus on pursuing negotiations, finding buyers, closing deals, and shutting down the bulk of Capital. There was no plan B for replacing the cash that GE would need to keep operating as it had. Plan B was to find it somehow, to figure it out.

In fact, Immelt, at least, thought he knew of one source of the cash they all craved. It had been set in motion months earlier, before any but a few executives had any inkling that the company planned to get rid of GE Capital. When the word had gone out in early 2014 from Fairfield to the business development teams embedded in the headquarters of all GE industrial business lines to send in their biggest M&A proposals, the bankers within the company all recognized the opportunity immediately. Jeff Immelt wanted to strike a really big deal.





31


Big Bets





THE NUMBER $30 loomed.

GE’s stock price and its miserable performance were a constant cloud over Immelt’s head.

He had transformed much of the company he inherited. He’d sold vestigial and beloved business units. He’d plunged deeper into entertainment, vastly expanded the global footprint. Delivering his perennial GE halftime orations—the football player’s rhetoric translated into exhortations about “winning” and “blocking and tackling” to hundreds of midlevel executives across the conglomerate—he was leaving his mark on the company.

And yet GE had been nearly destroyed by the habit that Jack Welch had developed and Jeff Immelt couldn’t kick. Finance had nearly brought them down. They all owned their reliance on it, even those who, like Immelt himself, didn’t fully appreciate the risks it exposed them to. And now that the financial crisis had passed, now that they had somehow survived it, GE Capital lay like a brick on the company’s spine.

Each infuriating news report about GE’s latest quarterly earnings contained some version of the same line, one the communications staff tried endlessly to argue out of articles, without success. It was a refrain that drifted like sour vapor among scores of current and retired executives whose compensation was tied up in stock options that were largely worthless.

It had been six years since a share of General Electric stock had fetched $30 on an open exchange, the stories all pointed out, much to the CEO’s frustration. Despite all Immelt’s changes and the company’s miraculous survival of the crisis, the stock persistently traded, not at the valuation of an industrial colossus, but like a mortgage company or a bank.

What the share price augured for Immelt wasn’t a Welch-like legacy. What it hinted at for a CEO who stood in constant, uneasy comparison to his legendary predecessor was a legacy that wasn’t even good. And though Immelt planned to stay in his position for years, time was running out to change the overarching narrative of his tenure.

Immelt knew that changing his legacy would require even more momentous changes in the architecture of the company. He needed to make moves that would finally impress upon Wall Street that he had found a way to lead the old General Electric into a new economic paradigm—causing its share price to respond accordingly.

Clearly, he’d have to lessen GE’s dependence on finance, but he would also need something, in one of his favorite words, “additive.” A master stroke to convince his critics that Jeff Immelt, just like Jack Welch, had the foresight and gumption to steer GE toward ever-greater profits. Immelt needed a big deal.

He would find his quarry in France. In the meantime, Immelt and GE continued doing what the company had done so readily under Welch: moving in and out of businesses, to the rhythm of a changing economy, as a protean corporation with an unchanging industrial core and an outer shell rigorously updated with the market trends and buzzwords of the moment.



Out in California, Bill Ruh was trying to convince a skeptical industry to believe a bold proposition: General Electric was a software company.

Ruh looked the part of the midcareer manager he was: with thinning brown hair and a sizable paunch, he was quick with a friendly chuckle. Like Immelt, he had a facility for the latest of the company’s buzzwords. At the moment that message was a single word: digital.

Jeff Immelt had seen the future of industrial companies: it was in software and hard-core computing. His vision was one that other industrial company leaders, including those who laughed at GE initially, would increasingly come to share. Immelt saw that, as technological innovation shrank the size and cost of digital sensors, they would be embedded in more and more machinery of every conceivable type. Huge streams of data could then be harvested, not just from smartphones and WiFi-enabled thermostats, but from the massive machines at the center of the developed economies around the world: gas-fired power turbines, jet engines on passenger aircraft, MRI machines and sonograms, the rumbling diesel-electric locomotives hauling freight across the American plains or down the core of the Indian subcontinent from Delhi to Bangalore.

There was support for Immelt’s foray into software in the traditionally gritty industrial world. Marc Andreessen famously wrote in 2011 that “software is eating the world,” meaning that it was transforming and disrupting businesses and sectors throughout the economy. But the widespread innovation, he noted, wouldn’t be as destructive for certain companies. “In some industries, particularly those with a heavy real-world component such as oil and gas, the software revolution is primarily an opportunity for incumbents,” he wrote. “Over the next 10 years, the battles between incumbents and software-powered insurgents will be epic.”

GE was determined to win that battle. To Immelt, his board of directors, and the executives who shared his vision, GE stood the greatest chance of harnessing these streams of industrial data. The company would know best which data to gather and how to interpret their meaning because they knew the machines; they had designed and built and repaired them for decades, after all. Reams of digital information were already being generated by the massive fleet of GE-designed jet engines that roared down runways each day. Working backward through the numbers, engineers empowered with the right digital tools could begin to learn new things from their own machines, Immelt said. “Big data” would help GE better predict when critical parts in engines and turbines were likely to wear out and break.

GE would then repackage that knowledge and sell it to customers. Service contracts could more reliably guarantee customers like airlines and utilities a minimum number of hours of smooth operation. Better planning for maintenance would flatten out and hold down labor costs for GE. And the digital model would provide critical “stickiness”—a guarantee against untold future innovations that might allow third-party businesses to swoop in and poach service contracts on GE-built machinery. That potential, which was already a problem in lower-tech industrial businesses like elevators and building systems, was a distant but real threat for all industrial businesses. It was in servicing industrial machinery—not in selling it—that all the profit lay.

Immelt would later tell the press that this revelation came to him in the aftermath of the financial crisis, on a visit to a GE jet engine factory where he heard engineers talking about the sensors that would be fitted onto the most modern engines. Where, the CEO wondered, was all that data going?

By 2011, GE had hired Ruh from Cisco and begun building the campus at San Ramon that would become GE Digital.

A few years after that, “digital” suffused the company’s vast marketing operation, the adjective having swollen to crowd out all others in everything from Immelt’s public speeches to the PR work of his top lieutenant, Beth Comstock. The communications office revised the boilerplate attached to every press release, the corporation’s description of itself, to pronounce GE the world’s first “digital-industrial” company. Immelt proclaimed in 2014 that GE would be a “top 10 software company” by 2020, with $4 billion in annual revenue from its new Predix software alone.

Spokesmen pitched stories about Immelt’s burgeoning appreciation for respected consultants and writers from Silicon Valley, like Eric Ries. In a move that owed much to Welch’s example, GE adapted the tech industry principles in Ries’s The Lean Startup to its industrial business with FastWorks, an internal program that would enable a conglomerate full of painstaking engineers to replicate the failure-friendly iterative research approach of major software companies.

But most important: Immelt green-lighted San Ramon. GE wasn’t cool in Silicon Valley. It wasn’t of the Valley. Its claim that it was hiring recruits away from Facebook and Oracle was a fine goal, but it was still an aspirational one, no matter what the company told investors and the press. The campus it was building, to house what would be called GE Digital, was in a dead end complex of office buildings located deep in a traffic-choked corridor thirty-five miles from downtown San Francisco called Bishop Ranch. The buildings themselves, though remodeled with exposed ducts and communal kitchens, were old offices masquerading as new ones. The office buildings had once housed the headquarters of the phone company Pacific Bell. According to local rumor, the complex had inspired the setting of the comic strip Dilbert.

Meanwhile, some of the tech innovations GE was producing were less real than they seemed. Back at the corporate headquarters in Fairfield, a High Impact Innovation Team (HIIT) was tasked with developing apps for use within the company—such as an iPad app to connect lighting engineers with the lighting sales force, or a web-based application for performance reviews. The innovations were dutifully pitched for small features in the business press by the division’s public relations staff. These pitches were, one employee said, “very friendly and bubbly and full of shit.”

In early 2014, the HIIT team received an urgent call: it was time to present one of the apps to Immelt, whose interest in tech was growing along with his public invocations of the “industrial internet” that GE would build for the world. But there was a small problem: the app in question wasn’t built yet. The team had digital design files—mock-ups of the way the program would eventually look—but nothing running on a real machine to show the CEO.

Supervisors waved away these concerns. It was time, they said, to “fake and bake.” Designers in Fairfield went to work animating flat visual designs, inserting enough movement to give the appearance, for one PowerPoint presentation, of an application that was working.

Word came back the next day. Immelt had loved it. (The app, in the end, went nowhere.)

In the meetings of the GE board of directors, all these machinations were regularly awarded a blessing of silence. The board, made up of current and retired business executives and academics, liked Immelt and didn’t want to challenge him. They were taken, as a group, by his gargantuan work ethic, his optimistic spirit, and his eagerness to articulate far-reaching visions about where the company’s strategy was taking them all.

It was no different with software, about which Immelt spoke with the zeal of the converted.

“What he says about software, in the boardroom anyway, is that it’s directionally the right place for GE to go,” a former director said.

“If GE is going to get where it needs to get,” Immelt would say, “then we’ve got to be serious about it. We got to go for it, guys. This isn’t a five-year spend; it’s ‘get in place as quickly as we can.’ ”

The GE board liked this kind of urgency, but didn’t usually force Immelt to commit to specifics. The board tacitly blessed Jeff Immelt’s digital dreams and his plans to stand up a software company inside General Electric, but never decided—much less voted on—a critical question about such a massive experiment: how much money were they going to spend on this?





32


Dinner in Paris





THE EFFORT TO execute Immelt’s pivot was still gathering force, which meant renewing the search for new sources of industrial revenue. The notion of buying Alstom, the French power equipment rival, had caught on quickly in Fairfield. But the rationale of the transaction, though it hit some thematic high notes, had been transformed and twisted as it wound its way upward through the layers of bureaucracy that weighed any major GE decision.

Just as GE had promised to redouble its efforts industrially, the deal represented a way for the company to invest in its oldest and largest businesses, the ones with the longest lead times and theoretically most stable returns. Alstom and GE were already something like second cousins, thanks to their shared historical connections to Thomson-Houston, the electric company that merged with Edison General Electric in 1892 to become General Electric. And to Immelt, a singular appeal of the deal was that it would give GE a dominant position compared to the other major players in the gas turbine market, especially Siemens and Mitsubishi.

When Immelt explained any given strategy or projected the company’s future performance, he continued to lean on athletic ideas of conquest. His competitive instincts in business drove him to such thinking. Like a football coach intent on picking up yards on the field, he liked taking market share, and one of his favorite tropes was promising that GE was poised to “win.” Buying Alstom would drive the other teams backward as GE claimed more and more of the field.

Furthermore, Alstom now seemed eager to make a deal. As GE teams mulled the possibility of an acquisition of the French conglomerate’s power units, Alstom’s imperious chief executive, Patrick Kron, reached out to Immelt. Would the American be willing to meet over dinner in Paris?

Whether Immelt fully recognized it or not, Kron was increasingly desperate to find a buyer for his company. Beneath the surface at Alstom, rot ran dangerously deep. Its power business was far behind GE and Siemens in bringing a new family of turbines to market. Worse still was the unprofitability of what it was already selling. The quiet capital raise that Adam Smith had noticed had turned out to be exactly the distress signal he had suspected. Alstom was dangerously low on cash.

Now its sell-side market analysts were getting suspicious.
