Welch was paid handsomely, partly because of his success and the skyrocketing stock, but he also locked in perks that would continue until after retirement, including a Manhattan apartment paid for by GE, access to corporate planes, sports and opera tickets, fresh flowers, and a personal chef. Welch himself would later surrender many of those perks when their disclosure in his divorce outraged the public and embarrassed the company. Nevertheless, some estimate that his wealth from working at GE climbed above $1 billion; the Wall Street Journal put it between $450 million and $800 million in 2002. His compensation for his last year at the company was valued at $122.5 million, the paper reported.

Executive compensation is difficult to measure because disclosure requirements weren’t in place until 2006. There is no doubt that Jeff Immelt also enjoyed plenty of perks, including heavy use of the corporate jets. As with many things around Immelt, there is contention around his enjoyment of perks versus him needing the required tools of an impossible job that never slowed down for sixteen years. Some claim he would eat only a chicken salad sandwich on the corporate jet; others claim the crew had to stock both cooked lobster and steak so Immelt had a choice while in flight. Some of these questions may never be settled.

Despite the dismal performance of the stock over his tenure, Immelt was paid handsomely, getting an estimated $168 million between 2006 and 2017 alone, according to the data firm Equilar. Jeff Bornstein’s four years as CFO brought an estimated $37 million, according to Equilar, while Keith Sherin collected compensation valued at $108 million from 2006 to 2016. That didn’t include Sherin’s time in the Welch years or his service as CFO since 1998.

In a modern corporate world where executives are regularly paid huge sums to lead companies, the payouts at GE are still jarring. What was left for retail shareholders—the “widows and orphans”—and the GE retirees? The value of their stakes in the company has in some cases been literally decimated. And the dividend checks on which so many expected to rely in their later years will now be counted not in dollars but in pennies.

But time has passed GE by. It will be a long road back to the company that had hordes of recent graduates applying for coveted internships and job applications from those hoping to follow in the footsteps of previous generations of their family who had thrived on the shop floor.

One former GE executive recently said that his daughter hadn’t even considered working at GE at the beginning of her professional career. “Are they even in the conversation anymore?” he asked.

In the end, Deirdre Latour had been asking the right question: what did Jeff Immelt—his tenure, his tactics, and his track record—actually mean? For that matter, what does GE itself mean now—to its stakeholders and to the society it helped to define? After all, any number of companies could sell machines to make power from natural gas or thrust American fighter planes up over open water.

The GE of the late twentieth century and the fitful first two decades of the twenty-first has stood for more than what it made. It has represented a capitalistic meritocracy, a locus not just of success but of a certain version of virtue—the virtue of targets made, goals surpassed, earnings earned, markets won. And it has stood for a vague but well-marketed notion that, in the unapologetic pursuit of a company’s fortunes, and one’s own, there is a certain uprightness—and a lesson for others.

But GE has stood for a well-bred hubris as well. Under Immelt, the company believed that the will to hit a target could supersede the math, even when hundreds of thousands of livelihoods—those of investors, customers, and suppliers, to say nothing of workers, retirees, and their families—hung in the balance. GE became synonymous with the idea that all that stood between a company and an arbitrary $2 earnings target was drive—simply wanting it bad enough.

Here the company’s aggressive accounting, macho culture, and reliance on competitive metaphor converged. Immelt’s greatest asset as a manager, even in his staunchest allies’ telling, came down to a football coach’s insistence on the prevailing power of gumption and guts. Even numbers were supposed to give way, like a tackle sled on a football practice field, if you cared enough, lowered your shoulder, and wanted it more than the next guy in line.

Where, Jeff Immelt used to ask from behind the dais in the Pit, is the guy I used to know? Your people don’t want it bad enough.

This was inspirational leadership—right up until it wasn’t.

Reality isn’t a tackle sled. Story isn’t a strategy. Sometimes imagination alone doesn’t work.




Where we have relied on background interviews in this book, we have operated as we would at the Journal. Details provided anonymously have been cross-checked against public records and interviews with other reliable sources and across multiple conversations. In certain cases, we have used pseudonyms to protect the identities of subjects and sources. We have taken into account the unreliability of memory, especially as some events have receded into the past. And we have remained attentive to the motives of those we interviewed—both on background and on the record—in their framing of the events that befell General Electric.
